8 min
The Prohibition of Giving Zakat to the Wealthy: Hanafi Position and Shafi'i Exception
Explains prohibition of giving zakat to the wealthy and rebuts Shafi'i's exception for warriors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to a wealthy person and explain why the Shafi'i exception for warriors is rejected.

Core Idea
Zakat is haram for any nisab-owner, even a warrior, because the prohibition is absolute.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical point in zakat disbursement: who is prohibited from receiving zakat? The matn lists categories, and the commentary expands on the prohibition of giving to a wealthy person. The central dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding warriors is then analysed. We will examine the evidence and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds it is haram to give zakat to anyone who owns nisab, regardless of their circumstances or status.
This is based on the hadith 'La tahill al-sadaqah li ghaniy'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i allows giving zakat to a wealthy warrior provided he does not receive state funding from the diwan or fay'.
The exception is based on a hadith from Ali listing five categories, including the warrior.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Examine the hadith of Ali that states sadaqah is permissible for the wealthy warrior, among others. Note that the Hanafi school questions its authenticity.
The hadith is not found in the standard collections according to Hanafi scholars.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand that the Hanafi school either rejects the Shafi'i hadith as unauthentic or interprets it differently (e.g., referring to physical strength rather than wealth). They also point out that the hadith does not include the condition about diwan.
The Hanafi response is based on the stronger, more general hadith of Mu'adh.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: In the Hanafi school, a warrior who owns nisab cannot receive zakat even if he lacks state income. In the Shafi'i school, he can.
This affects real-world distribution of zakat funds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المصرف
ومعتق البعض ونغني يملك نصاباً وعبده وطفله، وبني هاشم ومواليهم،
أي لا يدفع إلى غني فاضلا من الحوائج
ومعتق البعض: أي لا يدفع إلى عبده الذي أعتق بعضه؛ لأنه كالمكاتب عند الإمام، وعندهما: إذا أعتق بعضه كله فيصير أجنبياً. عتق عنه. (ع) وغني يملك نصاباً وفي بعض النسخ: "بملك نصاب" أي لا تدفع الزكاة إلى غني بسبب ملك نصاب مطلقاً أي نصاب كان حتى لو كان له خمس من الإبل أو أربعون من الغنم السائمة لا تحل له الصدقة، وقال الشافعي: يجوز دفع الزكاة إلى غني الغزاة إذا لم يكن له شيء في الديوان ولم يكن يأخذ من الفيء، وإنما قيد الغني بقوله: يملك نصابا؛ لأن الغني على ثلاث مراتب الأولى: ما يتعلق به وجوب الزكاة، والثانية: ما يتعلق به وجوب صدقة الفطر والأضحية، وهو ما يكون مالكاً لمقدار النصاب فاضلاً عن حوائجه الأصلية، وهو المراد هنا، والثالثة: ما يتعلق به تحريم السؤال، وهو أن يكون مالكاً لقوت يومه وما يستر به عورته عند عامة العلماء، ودليل الشافعي قوله علي: "لا تحل الصدقة لغني إلا لخمسة: الغازي في سبيل الله
والعامل على الصدقات والغارم ورجل اشترى الصدقة بماله ورجل له جار مسكين تصدق عليه فأهداها للغني". ولنا: ما روينا من حديث معاذ الله وقوله عليه: "لا تحل الصدقة لغني، وما رواه لم يصح، ولئن صح فهو محمول على الغني بقوة البدن والحديث مؤول بالإجماع؛ فإنه ليس فيه تقييد بأن لا يكون له شيء في الديوان و لم يأخذ من الفيء، فإذا حمله الشافعي على هذا حملناه على ما قلنا. (فتح)
Chapter of the Recipients. And the one who has partially freed a slave, and a wealthy person who owns nisab, and his slave and his child, and Banu Hashim and their mawali. That is, it is not given to a wealthy person who has surplus beyond his needs. And the one who has partially freed a slave: that is, it is not given to his slave whom he has partially freed because he is like a mukatab according to the Imam, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): when he has partially freed him, he becomes entirely free, so he becomes a stranger. (Ayni) And a wealthy person who owns nisab, and in some copies 'with ownership of nisab', meaning zakat is not given to a wealthy person due to owning any nisab, even if he has five camels or forty grazing sheep, sadaqah is not permissible for him. And al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible to give zakat to a wealthy warrior if he has nothing in the diwan and does not take from the fay'. He restricted the wealthy to one who owns nisab because wealth has three levels: first, what necessitates zakat; second, what necessitates sadaqat al-fitr and udhiyah, and that is one who owns the amount of nisab surplus to his original needs, and that is meant here; third, what prohibits asking, and that is one who owns his day's food and what covers his nakedness according to the general scholars. The evidence of al-Shafi'i is the saying of Ali: 'Sadaqah is not permissible for a wealthy person except for five: the warrior in the way of Allah, the worker on the sadaqat, the debtor, a man who bought sadaqah with his wealth, and a man who has a poor neighbor who gave him sadaqah and he gifts it to the wealthy.' And our evidence is what we narrated from the hadith of Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him) and his saying (peace be upon him): 'Sadaqah is not permissible for a wealthy person.' And what he narrated is not authentic, and if it were authentic, it is interpreted as referring to the wealthy in physical strength, and the hadith is interpreted by consensus; for there is no restriction in it that he has nothing in the diwan and does not take from fay'. So when al-Shafi'i interpreted it thus, we interpret it as we have said. (Fath)
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding giving zakat to a wealthy person. The matn states that zakat is not given to a wealthy person who owns nisab. The commentary clarifies that 'wealthy' here means one who owns nisab surplus to his basic needs, which is the second level of wealth (triggering sadaqat al-fitr). The text then presents the Shafi'i position that a wealthy warrior can receive zakat if he has no regular income from the state. The Hanafi response is based on the hadith of Mu'adh that sadaqah is not permissible for any wealthy person, and the Shafi'i proof is considered weak or interpreted restrictively. The Hanafi position is that the prohibition is absolute for anyone owning nisab.

Key Terms
غني (ghani): One who owns nisab surplus to basic needs (second level of wealth).
معتق البعض (mu'tiq al-ba'd): A slave partially freed; he is like a mukatab according to Abu Hanifa, so zakat cannot be given to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can zakat be given to a wealthy person who owns nisab but is fighting in jihad?
A) Yes, because jihad is a noble cause.
B) No, because zakat is prohibited for anyone who owns nisab.
C) Yes, if he has no other source of income.
D) No, unless he is also a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Zakat is absolutely prohibited for anyone who owns nisab, regardless of their role, based on the sound hadith.

Zakat to One's Slaves and Children: Eligibility Rules
Ruling on zakat to slave, child, and relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a person may or may not give zakat to their own slave, child, and other close relatives, and apply the exception for poor dependents.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot go to your own wealthy slave or child, but can go to a poor child or relative.

The source text first addresses a common scenario: May a person give zakat to his own slave or child? The answer is not a simple yes or no. It depends on the wealth status of the recipient. Furthermore, the ruling extends to other close relatives like the wife, father, and even the child of a wealthy woman. This lesson unpacks these conditional rulings so you can correctly identify which recipients are eligible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is giving zakat to one's own slave or child. The default ruling is prohibition if the slave or child is wealthy. Determine what 'wealthy' (ghani) means in this context.
Wealth is defined as possessing nisab or surplus beyond needs.
2
Apply the exception for poverty
If the child is an adult and poor (faqir), the prohibition lifts and giving zakat is permissible, even if the child's maintenance is on the father.
Poverty of the child is the decisive condition.
3
Extend to other close relatives
The same rule applies to one's wife, father, and the child of a wealthy woman: if they are poor, giving zakat to them is permissible.
The principle: need supersedes kinship in zakat distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و عبده وطفله: أي لا إلى عبد غني ولا إلى طفله، وقيد به؛ لأنه لو كان كبيرا فقيرا يجوز دفع الزكاة إليه وإن كان نفقته على الأب، وكذا امرأته وأبوه وطفل الغنية إذا كانوا فقراء يجوز الدفع إليهم. (مسكين)
وبني هاشم أي لا يجوز دفع الزكاة إلى بني هاشم لخبر البخاري: "نحن أهل بيت لا تحل لنا الصدقة"، وبنو هاشم آل علي وعباس وجعفر وعقيل وحارث بن عبد المطلب، وخصوا بالذكر؛ لأن بعض بني هاشم يعني بنى أبي لهب يجوز دفع الزكاة إليهم؛ لأن حرمة الصدقة كرامة لهم، وإنما استحقوها لنصرتهم النبي عليه في الجاهلية والإسلام ثم سرى ذلك إلى أولادهم، وأبو لهب آذى النبي الله فلا يستحق الكرامة، وكذا لا تدفع إلى موالي بني هاشم أي معتقيهم لما روي أنه علي بعث رجلاً من بني مخزوم على الصدقة فقال الرجل لأبي رافع مولى رسول الله : أصحبني كي ما يصيبك منها؟ فقال: حتى أسأل رسول الله له فانطلق فسأله فقال علي: "إن الصدقة لا تحل لنا، وإن مولى القوم من "أنفسهم" أي في حل الصدقة وحرمتها لا من جميع الوجوه، ألا ترى أنه ليس بكفء لهم وأن مولى المسلم إذا كان كافرا تؤخذ منه الجزية، ومولى التغلبي لا تؤخذ منه المضاعفة، ولا فرق الصدقة الواجبة والتطوع، وكذا الوقف لا يحل لهم، وقال بعض أصحابنا يحل لهم التطوع على وجه الصلة، والهاشمي لا يجوز له أن يدفع زكاته إلى هاشمي مثله عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لأبي يوسف. (فتح، مستخلص)
مواليهم: أي معتق بني هاشم، ويجوز النفل لهم على وجه الصلة. (ط،ع)
And his slave and his child: i.e., not to a wealthy slave nor to his (the giver's) child. And it is restricted because if he is an adult poor, it is permissible to give zakat to him even if his maintenance is on the father. Similarly, his wife, his father, and the child of a wealthy woman if they are poor, it is permissible to give to them. (Miskin) And Banu Hashim: i.e., it is not permissible to give zakat to Banu Hashim due to the hadith of Bukhari: 'We, the people of the house, sadaqah is not permissible for us.' And Banu Hashim are the family of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. They were specified because some of Banu Hashim, meaning the children of Abu Lahab, it is permissible to give zakat to them, because the prohibition of sadaqah is an honor for them, and they deserved it for supporting the Prophet (peace be upon him) in pre-Islam and Islam, and then that passed to their children. And Abu Lahab harmed the Prophet (peace be upon him), so he does not deserve honor. Similarly, it is not given to the mawali of Banu Hashim, i.e., their freed slaves, because it is narrated that Ali sent a man from Banu Makhzum to collect sadaqah, and the man said to Abu Rafi', the freed slave of the Messenger of Allah: 'Accompany me so that you may get some of it?' He said: 'Until I ask the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him).' He went and asked, and Ali said: 'Sadaqah is not permissible for us, and the freed slave of a people is from themselves' meaning in the permissibility and prohibition of sadaqah, not in all aspects. Do you not see that he is not their equal, and that the freed slave of a Muslim, if he is a disbeliever, jizya is taken from him, and the freed slave of a Taghlibi is not taken the double? And there is no difference between obligatory and voluntary sadaqah. Similarly, waqf is not permissible for them. And some of our companions say that voluntary sadaqah is permissible for them as a gift. And a Hashimi is not allowed to give his zakat to a like Hashimi according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf. (Fath, Mustakhlas) Their mawali: i.e., the freed slaves of Banu Hashim, and voluntary charity is permissible for them as a gift. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing those to whom zakat cannot be given. It explains that one cannot give zakat to one's own wealthy slave or wealthy child; however, if the child is poor, it is allowed even if the parent supports them. Similarly, one's wife, father, or child of a wealthy woman can receive zakat if they are poor. The second part covers Banu Hashim (the Prophet's clan) and their mawali (freed slaves). Zakat is prohibited for them due to honor, except for the descendants of Abu Lahab who opposed the Prophet. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa says a Hashimi cannot give his zakat to another Hashimi, while Abu Yusuf allows it. Voluntary charity (nafl) is allowed for Banu Hashim and their mawali as gifts.

Key Terms
عبد (abd): Slave; a person owned by another. In zakat, giving to one's own wealthy slave is prohibited.
طفل (tifl): Child; in this context, the child of the zakat giver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible to give zakat to one's own child?
A) When the child is wealthy and independent.
B) When the child is an adult and poor.
C) When the child is a minor and living with the parents.
D) It is never permissible.

Key Takeaway
Zakat may be given to one's own poor adult child and other poor close relatives, but not to wealthy dependents.

Zakat to Banu Hashim and Their Mawali: Prohibition and Exceptions
Ruling on zakat to Banu Hashim and their mawali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who among Banu Hashim is prohibited from receiving zakat, the exception for descendants of Abu Lahab, and the ruling regarding their mawali, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on Hashimi giving zakat to another Hashimi.

Core Idea
Zakat is prohibited for the noble clan of Banu Hashim and their freed slaves, except descendants of Abu Lahab, and voluntary charity is allowed as a gift.

The source text now turns to a significant ruling: zakat cannot be given to the Prophet's clan, Banu Hashim, as an honor and because they are the Ahl al-Bayt. However, not all descendants are included—those from Abu Lahab are an exception. Moreover, the freed slaves (mawali) of Banu Hashim also fall under the prohibition. An intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding a Hashimi giving zakat to another Hashimi is also mentioned. This lesson will help you navigate these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary prohibition
Zakat is prohibited for Banu Hashim. Learn the composition of Banu Hashim: the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. Understand the reason: it is an honor, and they were supporters of the Prophet.
The hadith from Bukhari: 'We, the people of the house, sadaqah is not permissible for us.'
2
Apply the exception for Abu Lahab's descendants
Abu Lahab opposed the Prophet, so his descendants do not share the honor and can receive zakat.
The text explicitly excludes them.
3
Extend to mawali (freed slaves)
The prohibition also applies to the freed slaves of Banu Hashim because 'the freed slave of a people is from themselves' in matters of sadaqah.
Evidence: the story of Ali and Abu Rafi'.
4
Distinguish between obligatory and voluntary charity
Obligatory sadaqah (zakat) is prohibited, but voluntary charity (nafl) as a gift (sila) is permissible for Banu Hashim and their mawali.
Some Hanafi scholars differ on voluntary charity; the text notes the view that it is allowed.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa says a Hashimi cannot give his zakat to another Hashimi; Abu Yusuf allows it. The text does not specify the mufta bihi, but the prohibition is the well-known position.
The dispute is about the giver, not the receiver: can a Hashimi pay zakat to another Hashimi?

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad including Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib.
موالي (mawali): Freed slaves of Banu Hashim; they also cannot receive zakat.
أبو لهب (Abu Lahab): The uncle of the Prophet who opposed him; his descendants are not included in the prohibition.

Key Takeaway
Zakat is forbidden for Banu Hashim and their freed slaves, except for Abu Lahab's line; voluntary charity is allowed as a gift.

Validity of Zakat Given by Ijtihad: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Discusses validity of zakat by ijtihad and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat given by ijtihad when the recipient later proves ineligible, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the proof from the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid.

Core Idea
Zakat given by ijtihad is valid even if the recipient is later found ineligible — intent and effort suffice.

This passage addresses a common concern: what if you give zakat to someone you believe is poor, but later discover they were wealthy or otherwise ineligible? The Hanafi school has an internal difference on this. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the payment valid, while Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i) consider it invalid. Let us examine the positions and their evidence, and understand the practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and basic ruling from the matn
Identify the act: giving zakat by ijtihad (taharrī). The matn says if the recipient turns out to be wealthy, Hashimi, disbeliever, father, or son, it is valid. But if the recipient is the giver's own slave or mukatab, it is not valid.
The distinction: some ineligibilities are excusable after ijtihad, others are not because ownership is not transferred.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two) say it is valid; Abu Yusuf says it is invalid because the error becomes certain. Al-Shafi'i agrees with Abu Yusuf.
Know the key figures: 'the two' (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) vs. Abu Yusuf.
3
Analyse the evidence: the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid
The hadith: 'For you is what you intended, O Yazid, and for you is what you took, O Ma'n.' It shows that the Prophet validated the charity despite the recipient not being the intended one, indicating that intent and effort suffice when the giver acts on his best judgment.
The hadith is used as a proof that ijtihad protects the validity of the payment.
4
Understand the distinction: why error after certainty invalidates
Abu Yusuf compares to wudu with water later found impure: the error becomes certain, so the act is invalid. The Hanafi response: ascertaining eligibility is by ijtihad without certainty; repeating would require another ijtihad, so no benefit. But if the recipient is the giver's slave, the payment does not leave his ownership, so it is invalid.
The key difference: some facts (like poverty) can only be approximated, while others (like slavery) can be known with certainty.
5
Note the practical consequence: mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the zakat is valid when given by ijtihad, even if the recipient later proves ineligible. This is the mufta bihi view.
The student should remember that in practice, zakat given in good faith after proper ijtihad is not to be repeated.

And if he gives by ijtihad, and it becomes apparent that he is wealthy, or a Hashimi, or a disbeliever, or his father, or his son, it is valid. But if it becomes apparent that he is his slave or his mukatab, it is not valid. And it is disliked to make him rich, and it is recommended to prevent him from asking, and it is disliked to transfer it to another town. By ijtihad: i.e., by exertion of opinion to a person who in his predominant view is a recipient. (Tahawi) Taharrī linguistically is seeking and desiring, and technically: seeking something by predominant conjecture when unable to ascertain its reality. And taharrī is not doubt nor conjecture: doubt is when the two sides of knowledge and ignorance are equal; conjecture is preponderance of one without evidence; taharrī is preponderance of one by predominant opinion, which is evidence by which one reaches knowledge, even if it does not reach what necessitates the reality of knowledge. (Fath) Or a disbeliever: i.e., a dhimmi, not a harbi, even if newly converted. (Tahawi) His father: i.e., the father of the giver, or his son, or his wife. (Tahawi) Its payment is valid according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said no. (Tahawi) i.e., its payment according to the two, and Abu Yusuf said it is not valid because his error became known with certainty, so it becomes like when one performs wudu with water or prays in a garment and it becomes apparent that it was impure, or a judge gives a verdict by ijtihad and then a text contrary to it appears. And al-Shafi'i also said this. And their evidence is what Bukhari narrated from Ma'n ibn Yazid that he said: 'My father Yazid took out dinars to give in charity and placed them with a man in the mosque. I came and took them and brought them to him. He said: By Allah, I did not intend you. So I disputed with him before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he said: For you is what you intended, O Yazid, and for you is what you took, O Ma'n.' And because ascertaining these matters is by ijtihad without certainty, and if we ordered him to repeat, he would be making ijtihad again, so there is no benefit, unlike the things... [text unclear] ...evidence; because he can ascertain them truly. And know that if the recipient is sitting with the poor, or is wearing their clothes, or he asked and he gave him, these reasons are like taharrī, so that if his wealth becomes apparent, it is not repeated. And the validity of payment in the case of taharrī is conditional upon his predominant view that he is. But if he doubted and did not make taharrī, or he made taharrī and paid while in his predominant view he is not a recipient, it is not permissible. (Fath, Miskin) And if it was his slave: i.e., if it becomes apparent that the recipient is the slave of the giver. And it is disliked to make him rich [i.e., it is not valid because it did not leave his ownership] It could mean the making rich that prohibits taking zakat, which is the purport of the author's absolute statement, so it is disliked to give goods equal to nisab. Or it could mean the making rich that necessitates zakat, then it is only disliked to give cash, i.e., it is disliked to give two hundred dirhams to one person. And Zufar said it is not permissible because wealth coincides with the payment, so the payment is made to a wealthy person and it is not permissible. And our evidence is that the payment meets the poor person because zakat is completed by ownership, and at the time of ownership he is poor, becoming wealthy after completion of ownership, so wealth is delayed from ownership necessarily. So it is permissible with dislike because it is adjacent to an invalidator, so it becomes like one who prays with impurity nearby. (Fath, Miskin) About asking: on this day, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Make them independent of asking on such a day.' (Ayni)

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of zakat given based on ijtihad (taharrī) when the recipient later turns out to be ineligible. The matn states that if one gives by ijtihad and the recipient is found to be wealthy, Hashimi, disbeliever, father, or son, the zakat is valid. But if it is found to be the giver's own slave or mukatab, it is not valid. The commentary defines taharrī and discusses the dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it valid, while Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i) say it is invalid because the error is certain by later revelation. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid where the Prophet validated the charity despite the recipient being unintended. The passage also discusses the dislike (karahah) of making a recipient rich by giving a large amount, and the recommendation to give enough to prevent asking. Transferring zakat to another town is disliked.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taharrī): Ijtihad based on predominant opinion to determine a valid recipient when uncertain.
الإغناء (al-ighna'): Making the recipient rich by giving a large amount, which is disliked (makruh).

The Validity of Zakat Payment by Ijtihad (Taharrī): The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of zakat paid by ijtihad when recipient is later found ineligible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat payment by ijtihad, identify the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the hadith proof to justify the majority position.

Core Idea
Zakat paid by ijtihad is valid even if the recipient is later found ineligible—intention and predominant opinion suffice.

This lesson examines a classic case within Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a Muslim pays zakat to someone he believes is eligible, but later discovers the recipient was actually rich, a Hashimi, a non-Muslim, or a close relative? The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq rules it valid, but this masks an important dispute among the early Hanafi masters. We will unpack the positions, the proofs, and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Define Taharrī and its role in zakat payment
Taharrī is seeking the correct recipient by predominant opinion when certainty is impossible. It is distinct from doubt and conjecture. Understand that taharrī is a valid method in Hanafi fiqh, especially in zakat distribution.
Refer to the Fath commentary in the source text for the distinction.
2
State the majority Hanafi ruling and identify the disputed case
The matn states: 'If he pays by ijtihad, and it becomes apparent that the recipient is rich, or a Hashimi, or a disbeliever, or his father or son, it is valid.' This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding the payment invalid.
The dispute is over the effect of later-discovered ineligibility.
3
Analyse the opposing view of Abu Yusuf and its analogy
Abu Yusuf analogizes to physical impurities: if one prays in a garment later found impure, the prayer is invalid. Similarly, here the ineligibility becomes certain, so the payment is void. Note that al-Shafi'i agreed with Abu Yusuf.
This is strict qiyas, not considering the nature of ijtihad.
4
Evaluate the hadith proof and the tarjih within the school
The majority cite the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid: 'You have what you intended, O Yazid.' This shows that the validity depends on the payer's intention, not the actual state. Since ascertaining eligibility is done by ijtihad, repeating would involve the same ijtihad—no benefit. The majority view is mufta bihi and preferred by later Hanafi authorities like al-Marghinani.
The tarjih is based on istihsan and the prophetic text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع بتحرّ، فبان أنه غني أو هاشمي أو كافر أو أبوه أوابنه صح
بتحر: [ أي باجتهاد إلى شخص وفي أكبر رأيه أنه مصرف (ط)] التحري لغة الطلب والابتغاء، وعرفاً: طلب الشيء بغالب الظن عند عدم الوقوف على حقيقته، والتحري غير الشك والظن، فالشك: أن يستوي طرفا العلم والجهل، والظن: ترجح أحدهما من غير دليل، والتحري ترجح أحدهما بغالب الرأي، وهو دليل يتوصل به إلى طرف العلم، وإن كان لا يتوصل به إلى ما يوجب حقيقة العلم. (فتح)
أو كافر: أي ذمي لا حربي ولو مستأنفاً (ط) أبوه أي أب المزكي أو ابنه أو زوجته. (ط)
صح دفعه عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف لا (ط)] أي دفعه عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يصح؛ لأن يصح. خطأه ظهر بيقين، فصار كما إذا توضأ بماء أو صلى في ثوب ثم تبين أنه كان نجساً، أو قضى القاضي باجتهاد ثم ظهر له نص بخلافه وبه قال الشافعي أيضاً، ولهما: ما رواه البخاري عن معن بن يزيد أنه قال: كان أبي يزيد أخرج دنانير يتصدق بها، فوضعها عند رجل في المسجد، فجئت فأخذتها فأتيته بها، فقال: والله ما إياك أردت، فخاصمته إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "لك ما نويت يا يزيد، ولك ما أخذت يا معن"؛ ولأن الوقوف على هذه الأشياء بالاجتهاد دون القطع، ولو أمرناه بالإعادة لكان مجتهدا فيه أيضاً، فلا فائدة فيه بخلاف الأشياء التي مصرف، استدل بها؛ لأنه يمكنه الوقوف عليها حقيقة. واعلم أن المدفوع إليه إذا كان جالسا مع الفقراء أو كان عليه زيهم أو سأله فأعطاه كانت هذه الأسباب بمنزلة التحري، حتى لو ظهر غناه لم يعد وصحة الدفع في صورة التحري مقيدة بما إذا كان في أكبر رأيه أنه . أما لو شك فلم يتحر أو تحرى فدفع وفي أكبر رأيه أنه ليس بمصرف لا يجوز. (فتح، مسكين)
And if he pays with ijtihad, and it becomes apparent that [the recipient] is rich, or a Hashimi, or a disbeliever, or his father or his son, it is valid. [Commentary:] Taharrī: linguistic meaning is seeking and searching; technical meaning: seeking something by predominant conjecture when its reality cannot be ascertained. Taharrī is different from doubt and conjecture. Doubt: both sides of knowledge and ignorance are equal. Conjecture: one side outweighs but without evidence. Taharrī: one side outweighs by predominant opinion, and it is a means to reach knowledge, even if it does not yield certain knowledge. (Fath) 'Or a disbeliever' means a dhimmi, not a harbi, even if newly engaged (in contract). (T) 'His father' means the father of the payer, or his son, or his wife. (T) 'It is valid upon payment according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said no.' Meaning his payment is valid according to the two parties, and Abu Yusuf said it is not valid, because his error has become certain, so it is like one who performed wudu with water or prayed in a garment then it became apparent that it was impure, or a judge who ruled by ijtihad then found a text contrary to it; and al-Shafi'i also said this. Their proof: what Bukhari narrated from Ma'n ibn Yazid who said: My father Yazid took out dinars to give in charity and placed them with a man in the mosque. I took them and brought them to him. He said, 'By Allah, I did not intend you.' So I disputed with him before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he said: 'You have what you intended, O Yazid, and you have what you took, O Ma'n.' And because ascertaining these matters is by ijtihad, not certainty, and if we ordered him to repeat, he would also be exercising ijtihad, so there is no benefit. Unlike matters that are clear, evidence is used because they can be ascertained truly. Know that if the recipient is sitting among the poor, or is dressed like them, or asks, and he gives him, these causes take the place of ijtihad, so even if his wealth becomes apparent, he does not repeat. The validity of payment in the case of ijtihad is conditioned on his predominant opinion that he is an eligible recipient. As for if he doubted and did not exercise ijtihad, or he exercised ijtihad and paid while his predominant opinion was that he is not eligible, it is not permissible. (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on paying zakat based on ijtihad (taharrī) when the payer does not know the recipient's eligibility with certainty. The ruling is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq: if a person pays zakat by exercising ijtihad (i.e., making a reasoned effort to determine a deserving recipient), and it later becomes apparent that the recipient was not eligible—either because he is rich, or a Hashimi (descendant of the Prophet), or a non-Muslim (dhimmi), or the payer's father, son, or wife—the payment is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two). Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that since the ineligibility is certain, the payment is invalid, analogizing it to praying in a garment that turns out to be impure. The author presents the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid from Bukhari as proof for the majority: the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Yazid, 'You have what you intended,' indicating that the intention suffices when the actual state is unknown. The commentary further notes that the ijtihad must be genuine: if the payer had a predominant opinion that the person was eligible, it is valid; if he paid without ijtihad or despite thinking the person was ineligible, it is not valid. The passage also mentions that circumstances like the recipient sitting among the poor or asking serve as evidence of eligibility, equivalent to ijtihad. This is a clear intra-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school, with the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) relying on the hadith and istihsan (juristic preference), while Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i prioritize certainty.

Key Terms
تحري (taharrī): Seeking the correct recipient of zakat by using predominant opinion (ghalabah al-zann) when the reality is not known with certainty.
غني (ghani): A person who possesses the nisab (minimum wealth) or more, making them ineligible to receive zakat.
هاشمي (hashimi): A descendant of Hashim, the Prophet's great-grandfather; such persons cannot receive zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling if a person pays zakat based on ijtihad and later discovers the recipient was rich?
A) The payment is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The payment is valid and counts towards zakat.
C) The payment is valid only if the recipient was not a relative.
D) The payment is suspended until the recipient's status is confirmed.

Key Takeaway
Zakat paid by genuine ijtihad (predominant opinion) is valid even if the recipient is later found ineligible, per the majority of Hanafi scholars, based on the hadith of Ma'n ibn Yazid.

Paying Zakat to One's Slave or Making a Recipient Rich: Rulings and Reasoning
Rulings on paying zakat to one's own slave and on ighna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on paying zakat to one's own slave/mukatib and on making the recipient rich (ighna'), and explain the legal reasoning behind the permissibility with dislike.

Core Idea
Paying zakat to one's own slave is invalid; making a recipient rich is disliked but valid because richness occurs after ownership transfers.

In this lesson, we examine two important rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. First, if the recipient of zakat turns out to be the payer's own slave or mukatib, the payment is invalid because the property never truly leaves the payer's ownership. Second, it is disliked (makruh) to give an amount that makes the recipient reach the level of being rich, though the payment itself is valid due to the timing of ownership transfer. We also explore the minority view of Imam Zafar, who considered such payments absolutely invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings from the matn
The matn states two rulings: (1) paying zakat to one's own slave or mukatib is invalid; (2) it is disliked (kariha) to make the recipient rich (ighna') and also to transfer zakat to another city. Focus on the first two.
The commentary expands on the reasons and exceptions.
2
Understand the invalidity of paying to one's own slave
The commentary explains that if the recipient is the payer's slave, the payment does not leave the payer's ownership because the slave and his property belong to the payer. Hence the zakat has not been delivered to a valid recipient.
This applies to mukatib as well, because he remains owned.
3
Analyze the ruling on ighna' (making rich)
The matn says it is disliked. Two interpretations: (a) disliked to give goods worth a nisab (making rich for the first time) because then the recipient becomes ineligible for future zakat; (b) disliked only when paying from cash (nagd), e.g., 200 dirhams to one person.
The dislike is tanzihi (not tahrimi) according to the majority.
4
Consider the ikhtilaf with Zafar
Imam Zafar argues that if the payment makes the recipient rich, the payment coincides with richness and is thus invalid. The majority respond: at the moment of transfer (tamlik), the recipient is poor; richness occurs after completion, so the payment is valid but disliked.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the relied-upon view is permissibility with dislike.
5
Apply the principle of transfer of ownership
The key legal principle: zakat is completed by tamlik (transfer of ownership). As long as the recipient owns the property when the transfer is made, the payment is valid even if he becomes rich immediately after.
Analogy: prayer with impurity nearby is valid but disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو عبده: أي لو ظهر أن المرفوع إليه عبد المزكي. وكره الإغناء [ أي لا يصح؛ لأنه لم يخرج من ملكه ] يمكن أن يكون المراد الإغناء المحرم لأخذ الزكاة، وهو مقتضى إطلاق المصنف فيكره دفع عرض يساوي نصاباً، وإن يكون المراد الإغناء الموجب للزكاة فلا يكره إلا الدفع من النقد، أي يكره أن يدفع إلى واحد مائتي درهم وقال زفر : لا يجوز؛ لأن الغنى قارن الأداء، فحصل الأداء للغني وهو لا يجوز ولنا أن الأداء يلاقي الفقير؛ لأن الزكاة إنما تتم بالتمليك وهو حالة التمليك فقير، غنياً بعد تمام التمليك، فيتأخر الغني عن التمليك ضرورة، فجاز مع الكراهة؛ لأنه جاور المفسد، فصار وإنما يصير كمن صلى وبقربه نجاسة. (فتح، مسكين)
And if his slave: meaning if it becomes apparent that the recipient is the payer's own slave. And it is disliked to make [the recipient] rich: [meaning it is not valid because it did not leave his ownership]. It is possible that what is meant is the enrichment that prohibits taking zakat, which is the implication of the author's absolute expression, so it is disliked to give goods worth a nisab. And if what is meant is enrichment that makes zakat obligatory, then it is disliked only when paying from cash, i.e., it is disliked to pay two hundred dirhams to one person. Zafar said: it is not permissible because the wealth (ghana) coincided with the payment, so the payment was made to a rich person, which is not permissible. Our evidence: the payment meets the poor [at the time of possession], because zakat is completed only by transfer of ownership (tamlik), and at the moment of transfer he is poor; he becomes rich after the transfer is complete. Therefore, the richness follows the transfer by necessity, so it is permissible with dislike, because it bordered on a corrupting factor, and it becomes like someone who prays while there is impurity nearby. (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage covers two separate rulings from the matn: first, if it becomes apparent that the recipient of zakat is the payer's own slave (or mukatib, a slave contracted to purchase freedom), the payment is not valid (or as the commentary says, it is disliked? Actually the matn said 'la' meaning no, it is not valid. The commentary explains that if the recipient turns out to be the payer's slave, the payment is not valid because the payer cannot own his own property through zakat. Second, the ruling on making the recipient rich (ighna') is disliked (makruh). The commentary clarifies different interpretations: according to some, it is disliked to give an amount that makes the recipient rich (e.g., giving goods worth a nisab), because the recipient then becomes ineligible for future zakat. According to another interpretation, it is disliked only when paying from cash (nagd) i.e., giving two hundred dirhams (the nisab for silver) to a single person. Zafar, a later Hanafi scholar, says it is not permissible at all, arguing that the payment coincides with the recipient's richness. The mainstream Hanafi position is that it is permissible but disliked, because at the moment of transfer, the recipient is considered poor (since ownership transfers at that instant), and richness occurs after completion. The analogy is given of someone praying while impurity is nearby—the prayer is valid but disliked. The passage also implies that it is disliked to transfer zakat to another city (mentioned in the matn as 'kariha naqlaha ila balad akhar'), but the commentary here does not expand on that.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
إغناء (ighna'): Giving an amount of zakat that makes the recipient reach the level of being rich (i.e., owning nisab).
نقد (naqd): Cash money, specifically gold or silver currency (dirhams and dinars).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person pays zakat to his own slave?
A) Valid, because the slave is a poor person.
B) Invalid, because the property does not leave the payer's ownership.
C) Permissible with dislike.
D) The slave cannot own property, so it is invalid only if the slave is not a mukatib.

Key Takeaway
Paying zakat to one's own slave or mukatib is invalid; making a recipient rich is disliked but valid, and this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Preferring Relatives and Prohibition of Asking in Zakat Context
Ruling statement on zakat distribution order and prohibition of begging

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on preferring relatives in zakat distribution and identify when asking for charity is prohibited, citing the hadith and its conditions.

Core Idea
Give zakat to poor relatives first; do not ask if you have food for the day.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary addresses two important zakat-related rulings: the preference for giving to one's needy relatives and the prohibition of begging for those who have sufficient food. Understanding these rulings helps ensure zakat fulfills both the social and spiritual objectives of the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal rulings in the passage
The passage states a recommendation to give zakat to relatives (start with brothers, then their children, then paternal uncles, etc.) and a prohibition on asking for charity for those who have provision for the day.
The rulings are distinct but connected in the context of zakat distribution.
2
Examine the preferred order of recipients
List the order: poor brothers, their children, paternal uncles, maternal uncles, other relatives, neighbors, residents. Note that the location of wealth is considered for zakat, with a difference for zakat al-fitr.
The priority aims to combine sadaqah with silat al-rahim.
3
Analyze the prohibition of asking
The hadith 'Whoever asks while he has what suffices him, accumulates embers of hell' defines sufficiency as having lunch and dinner. The prohibition applies to asking for food items; other needs like clothing are permissible.
Additional conditions: owning 50 dirhams, an uqiyya, or being healthy and able to earn also trigger prohibition.
4
Understand the scope of the prohibition
The prohibition is for asking for what one does not need. If in genuine need, asking is allowed. The giver sins if he knows the asker has sufficient provision.
This reinterpretation reconciles hadith that seem absolute.
5
Apply the rulings to real scenarios
Consider a case: a healthy employed person asks for food. According to the passage, this is prohibited. If a relative is poor and needy, giving zakat to them is preferred over a non-relative.
The mufta bihi is that it is mustahab to give to poor relatives and haram to ask when sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لغير قريب وأحوج، ولا يسأل من له قوت يومه.
أي له أي لا يحل له السؤال أي غداؤه وعشاؤه
جيرا لغير قريب وأحوج لأن المقصود سد خلة المحتاج، وفي القريب جمع بين الصلة والصدقة حتى قيل: لا تقبل صدقة الرجل وقرابته محاويج حتى يبدأ بهم ويسد حاجتهم والأفضل صرف الصدقة إلى إخوته الفقراء، ثم أولادهم، ثم أعمامه الفقراء، ثم أخواله، ثم ذوي أرحامه، ثم جيرانه ثم أهل سكنه ويعتبر في الزكاة مكان المال، واختلف في صدقة الفطر، فقيل: مكان الرأس، وقيل: مكان من تجب عليه. (فتح) وأحوج من أهل بلده أو أورع أو أصلح أو أنفع للمسلمين أو إلى طالب علم أو من دار الحرب إلى دار الإسلام. (ط) ولا يسأل: لقوله : " من سأل وعنده ما يغنية فإنما يستكثر جمر جهنم، قالوا: يا رسول الله! ما يغنيه؟ قال: "ما يغديه ويعشيه". فالقدرة على الغداء والعشاء تحرم سؤال الغداء والعشاء، ويجوز معها سؤال الجبة والكساء، وجاء في الخبر حرمة السؤال على من يملك خمسين درهماً وروي على من يملك أوقية، وعلى من يكون صحيحا مكتسباً. [تبيين الحقائق: ۱۳۱/۲] وحرمة السؤال في هذه الأخبار محمولة على سؤال ما لا يحتاج إليه، وقوله: "من له قوت يومه أي بالفعل أو بالقوة كالصحيح المكتسب، يأثم معطيه إن علم بحاله؛ لإعانته على المحرم. (فتح)
To other than a relative and more needy, and he should not ask who has provision for his day. [Commentary:] Meaning: he does not permissible for him to ask; meaning his lunch and dinner. Therefore, to other than a relative and more needy, because the purpose is to fill the need of the needy. In the relative, there is combining both maintaining ties and charity, so it is said: the charity of a man is not accepted while his relatives are needy until he begins with them and fills their need. It is best to give charity to his poor brothers, then their children, then his poor paternal uncles, then his maternal uncles, then his blood relatives, then his neighbors, then the people of his residence. In zakat, the location of the wealth is considered. There is disagreement about zakat al-fitr: some say the location of the head, others say the location of the one obliged. (Fath) And more needy from his town, or more pious, or more righteous, or more beneficial to Muslims, or to a student of knowledge, or from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam. (T) And he should not ask: because he (peace be upon him) said: "Whoever asks while he has what suffices him, he only accumulates embers of hell." They said: O Messenger of Allah, what suffices him? He said: "What feeds him for lunch and dinner." So the ability to have lunch and dinner prohibits asking for lunch and dinner. It is permissible with it to ask for a cloak and clothing. And in the report: asking is prohibited for one who owns fifty dirhams, and it is narrated for one who owns an uqiyya, and for one who is healthy and able to earn. (Tabyin al-Haqaiq 131/2) The prohibition of asking in these reports is interpreted as asking for what one does not need. And his saying: 'who has provision for his day' means actually or potentially, like a healthy earner. The giver sins if he knows his condition, because he helps him in the prohibited act. (Fath)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage, from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary, covers two main themes: the preference for giving zakat to relatives and the prohibition of asking for those who have sufficient provision. The author states that it is better to give zakat to non-relatives who are more needy (لغير قريب وأحوج) — but the commentary clarifies that this does not mean relatives are neglected; rather, the ideal is to first fulfill the needs of one's own poor relatives, because it combines charity with maintaining family ties. The preference order is: poor brothers, then their children, then paternal uncles, then maternal uncles, then more distant relatives, then neighbors, then people of the same residence. For zakat, the location of the wealth (where the wealth is located) is considered for distribution. For zakat al-fitr, there is a difference of opinion: some say it should be given where the person is (head), others where the obligation is. The commentary also adds that it is more rewarding to give to someone from one's own town who is more needy, more pious, more beneficial to Muslims, or to a student of knowledge, or to someone migrating from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam. The second part prohibits asking for charity from those who have food for their day (قوت يومه). This is derived from a hadith: 'Whoever asks while he has what suffices him, he only accumulates embers of hell.' The sufficiency is defined as having food for lunch and dinner. This makes asking for such food prohibited, but asking for other items like clothing is permissible. The prohibition also applies to those who own fifty dirhams or an ounce (uqiyya) of silver, or who are healthy and able to earn. The commentary emphasizes that the prohibition is for asking for what one does not truly need; if one is in genuine need, asking is allowed. The giver who knows the beggar has sufficient provision commits a sin by enabling the prohibited act.

Key Terms
قريب (qarib): Relative; the commentary specifies poor brothers, their children, paternal uncles, maternal uncles, etc.
قوت يومه (qut yawmihi): Food that suffices for the day, specifically lunch and dinner.
اكتساب (iktisab): Ability to earn a livelihood through work; a healthy person capable of earning is considered like one who has provision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this passage, what constitutes having sufficient provision to prohibit asking for charity?
A) Having a home and property.
B) Having food for lunch and dinner.
C) Having a stable job.
D) Having 50 dirhams in savings.

Key Takeaway
Give zakat to needy relatives first to combine charity with family ties, and do not ask for charity if you have food for the day or can earn it.

Priority in Zakat Disbursement: Relatives or Non-Relatives?
Ruling on zakat disbursement priorities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the priority of zakat disbursement to needy relatives versus non-relatives, and apply the order of preference in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Zakat to non-relatives is preferred, but needy relatives take precedence.

The chapter on disbursement (masraf) addresses to whom zakat should be given. The primary ruling is to give to non-relatives who are more needy, as this fulfills the purpose of zakat—filling the need of the needy. However, the text also emphasizes that if one's own relatives are in need, they take priority because giving to them combines charity with maintaining family ties. This lesson explains the logic behind this priority and the recommended order of recipients.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening phrase: 'لغير قريب وأحوج' (To a non-relative and more needy). This is the default preference.
2
Recognize the exception for needy relatives
The commentary explains that charity is not accepted from a man while his relatives are in need until he begins with them. Thus, relatives in need take priority over non-relatives.
3
Understand the reasoning
The text states that giving to relatives combines 'صلة' (maintaining ties) with 'صدقة' (charity). This dual benefit makes it superior.
4
Apply the order of preference
The commentary lists a hierarchy: poor brothers, their children, paternal uncles, maternal uncles, relatives, neighbors, then residents of the same dwelling. Also, factors like piety, benefit to Muslims, or being a student of knowledge increase priority.
These are recommendations, not obligations.
5
Consider additional criteria
The text adds that the most needy (أحوج) from one's town, or the more pious, righteous, beneficial, or a student of knowledge, or one migrating from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam, may take precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المصرف
لغير قريب وأحوج، ولا يسأل من له قوت يومه.
أي له
أي لا يحل له السؤال
أي غداؤه وعشاؤه

لغير قريب وأحوج لأن المقصود سد خلة المحتاج، وفي القريب جمع بين الصلة والصدقة حتى قيل: لا تقبل صدقة الرجل وقرابته محاويج حتى يبدأ بهم ويسد ،حاجتهم والأفضل صرف الصدقة إلى إخوته الفقراء، ثم أولادهم، ثم أعمامه الفقراء، ثم أخواله، ثم ذوي أرحامه، ثم رانه ثم أهل سكنه ويعتبر في الزكاة مكان المال، واختلف في صدقة الفطر، فقيل: مكان الرأس، وقيل: مكان من تجب عليه. (فتح) وأحوج من أهل بلده أو أورع أو أصلح أو أنفع للمسلمين أو إلى طالب علم أو من دار الحرب إلى دار الإسلام. (ط) ولا يسأل: لقوله : " من سأل وعنده ما يغنيه فإنما يستكثر جمر جهنم، قالوا: يا رسول الله! ما يغنيه؟ قال: "ما يغديه ويعشيه". فالقدرة على الغداء والعشاء تحرم سؤال الغداء والعشاء، ويجوز معها سؤال الجبة والكساء، وجاء في الخبر حرمة السؤال على من يملك خمسين درهماً وروي على من يملك أوقية، وعلى من يكون صحيحا مكتسباً. [تبيين الحقائق: ۱۳۱/۲] وحرمة السؤال في هذه الأخبار محمولة على سؤال ما لا يحتاج إليه، وقوله: "من له قوت يومه أي بالفعل أو بالقوة كالصحيح المكتسب، يأثم معطيه إن علم بحاله؛ لإعانته على المحرم. (فتح)
Chapter of Disbursement: To a non-relative and more needy, and one who has the sustenance of his day should not ask. (Explanation: 'for him' meaning it is not permissible for him to ask; 'his lunch and dinner'; 'neighbors') To a non-relative and more needy because the purpose is to fill the need of the needy, and in the relative there is joining of kinship and charity, so much so that it was said: The charity of a man is not accepted while his relatives are in need until he begins with them and fulfills their need. It is better to give charity to his poor brothers, then their children, then his poor paternal uncles, then his maternal uncles, then his relatives, then his neighbors, then the people of his dwelling. In zakat, the location of the wealth is considered, and there is disagreement about sadaqat al-fitr: it was said the location of the head, and it was said the location of the one upon whom it is obligatory. (Fath) And more needy from the people of his town, or more pious, or more righteous, or more beneficial to Muslims, or to a student of knowledge, or from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam. (Tahawi) And he should not ask: because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever asks while he has what suffices him, he only accumulates embers of Hell.' They said: O Messenger of Allah, what suffices him? He said: 'What gives him his morning and evening meal.' So ability to have breakfast and dinner prohibits asking for breakfast and dinner, but asking for a cloak and clothing is permissible with it. It came in the report the prohibition of asking for one who owns fifty dirhams, and it was narrated for one who owns an uqiyya, and for one who is healthy and earning. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 2/131] And the prohibition of asking in these reports is interpreted as asking for what one does not need. And his saying: 'one who has the sustenance of his day' i.e., actually or potentially like the healthy earner; the giver sins if he knows his condition because he aids in what is prohibited. (Fath)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the chapter of disbursement (masraf) of zakat. The main ruling is that zakat should be given to non-relatives who are more needy, rather than relatives, because giving to relatives combines both charity and maintaining family ties. However, if the giver's own relatives are in need, it is better to start with them. The passage then discusses the prohibition of asking for alms: anyone who has sufficient food for the day (morning and evening meals) is forbidden to ask for money for food, though they may ask for clothing. The hadith cited (from al-Tirmidhi) sets the threshold as having enough for the day's meals. Several reports mention specific amounts (50 dirhams, one uqiyya) but these are understood as examples. The prohibition applies to asking for what one does not need. A healthy person capable of earning is considered as having sustenance potentially and thus should not ask. The giver who knows the asker's condition sins by aiding him in a forbidden act. The commentary also mentions that in zakat, location of wealth matters, and for sadaqat al-fitr there is a difference of opinion.

Key Terms
قوت يومه (qūt yawmih): Sustenance for one day; specifically morning and evening meals (ghada' and 'asha').
سؤال (su'āl): Asking for alms or charity; prohibited when one has daily sustenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the first category of relatives that should receive zakat if they are needy?
A) Paternal uncles
B) Maternal uncles
C) Brothers
D) Neighbors

Key Takeaway
Needy relatives take precedence over non-relatives in zakat disbursement due to the combined benefit of charity and family ties.

Prohibition of Asking for Alms: When Does One Have Sufficient Sustenance?
Ruling on prohibition of begging with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when asking for alms becomes prohibited (haram) according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between asking for food and asking for clothing.

Core Idea
Asking for food is haram if you have enough for the day; asking for clothing is permissible.

The Prophet (peace be upon him) warned against asking for charity when one has sufficient means. But what exactly constitutes sufficiency? The hadith clarifies that it is having enough for the morning and evening meals. This lesson examines the Hanafi interpretation of this threshold and explores related rulings, including the permissibility of asking for clothing and the responsibility of the giver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'su'al' (asking for alms). The ruling is prohibition (haram) for one who has sufficient daily sustenance.
2
Understand the definition of sufficiency
The hadith defines sufficiency as having what gives one his morning and evening meal (ghada' and 'asha'), i.e., food for one day.
3
Differentiate between asking for food and clothing
The text explicitly states: 'the ability to have breakfast and dinner prohibits asking for breakfast and dinner, but asking for a cloak and clothing is permissible with it.'
4
Consider other thresholds mentioned
The text also mentions reports about owning 50 dirhams or one uqiyya, but these are interpreted as examples; the core criterion is daily sustenance.
5
Note the giver's liability
The giver sins if he knows the asker has sufficient sustenance, as he aids in a prohibited act.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Status and Conditions of Sadaqat al-Fitr
Defines obligation and conditions of Sadaqat al-Fitr with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Sadaqat al-Fitr (wajib), identify the conditions for its obligation (freedom, Islam, nisab), and distinguish it from fard according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib, not fard, because its proof is ahad; unlike zakat, its nisab does not require growth.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. It establishes that this charity is wajib upon a free Muslim who possesses surplus wealth beyond essential needs. The commentator clarifies the conditions, the proof, and distinguishes the Hanafi view from al-Shafi'i and even from an internal Hanafi disagreement regarding minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Sadaqat al-Fitr is classified as wajib (obligatory) in the Hanafi school, not fard. Note that this is the core ruling established in the matn.
The text explicitly says 'تجب' (it is obligatory) and then contrasts with al-Shafi'i who says it is fard.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof cited is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (مما فرض رسول الله زكاة الفطر) and the statement 'لا صدقة إلا عن ظهر غنى' (No charity except from surplus wealth). These are ahad hadith, not mutawatir.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud; the Hanafi school uses it to establish wujub, not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative form in 'أدوا' (pay) indicates obligation according to Hanafi usul; the exclusionary phrase 'لا صدقة إلا عن ظهر غنى' establishes that charity is only due from surplus wealth, which is the condition for nisab.
The wajh al-dalalah is that the ahad transmission yields zanni al-thubut, hence the ruling is at the level of wajib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Two ikhtilafat are mentioned: (1) Inter-madhhab: al-Shafi'i considers it fard because he views the hadith as qat'i. (2) Intra-Hanafi: Imam Muhammad says it is not wajib on a minor; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is wajib.
The text uses 'هما يقولان' (they two say) to indicate the majority view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib on the free Muslim with nisab, including on behalf of minors under his care. The majority view is adopted.
The tarjih is implicit; the text sides with the pair against Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة باب صدقة الفطر باب صدقة الفطر في أي في بيان أحكامها على حرّ مسلم ذي نصاب فضل عن مسكنه وثيابه وأثاثه وفرسه وسلاحه احترز به عن الرقيق أي صاحب وعبيده عن نفسه. أي يجب إخراجها أي وفضل عن ثيابه أي متاعه صدقة الفطر من قبيل إضافة الشيء إلى شرطه، وإنما قدمت على الصوم مع أنها تجب بعده؛ لأنها عبادة مالية كالزكاة والفطر بالكسر كلمة مولدة لا عربية ولا معربة، بل هي اصطلاح للفقهاء فيكون حقيقة شرعية، والصدقة: العطية التي يراد بها المثوبة، وركنها الأداء إلى المصرف وسبب شرعيتها ما جاء في حديث ابن عباس ما: فرض رسول الله زكاة الفطر طهرة للصائم من اللغو والرفث وطعمة للمساكين، من أداها قبل الصلاة فهي أداها زكاة مقبولة، ومن بعد الصلاة فهي صدقة الصدقات أمر بها قبل العيد بيومين قبل أن تفرض زكاة من المال وهو الصحيح، والصوم والزكاة فرضا في السنة الثانية من الهجرة وكذا تحويل القبلة. (فتح) تجب: أي صدقة الفطر خلافاً للشافعي فإنها عنده فرض . (ع) على حر :مسلم شرط الحرية؛ ليتحقق التمليك، وشرط الإسلام؛ لتقع الصدقة ،قربة وقال محمد: لا تجب على الصغير؛ لأنها عبادة، وهما يقولان: فيها معنى المؤنة؛ عن لأنه يتحملها عن الغير، فصارت كنفقة الأقارب، بخلاف الزكاة؛ لأنها عبادة محضة؛ ولهذا لا يتحملها أحد . أحد، وقوله: "ذي نصاب" لا يعتبر فيه وصف النماء، وقال الشافعي: تجب على من يملك زيادة عن قوت يومه؛ لحديث ابن عباس لما المتقدم، ولنا قوله عليه: "لا صدقة إلا عن ظهر غنى"، وقوله: "فضل عن مسكنه"؛ لأن المشغول بالحاجة الأصلية كالمعدوم ومن حوائجه الأصلية دار السكونة حتى لو كان له داران دار يسكنها، ودار أخرى لا يسكنها يؤاجرها أو لا تعتبر قيمتها في الغنى حتى لو كانت قيمتها مائتي درهم تجب عليه صدقة الفطر، وكذا يكون فاضلاً ثيابه ومتاع البيت والفرس والسلاح التي تستعمل للحاجة الدينية وعبيد الخدمة؛ ولهذا قالوا: إن كتب التفسير والفقه والمصحف الواحد لا يكون نصاباً؛ لكونها مشغولة بالحاجة الأصلية الدينية، وأما كتب النحو والأدب والطب والتعبير فتعتبر نصاباً. (مسكين) مسلم: احترز به عن الكافر سواء كان المسلم صغيراً أو كبيراً أو مجنوناً حتى لو لم يخرجها الولي وجب الأداء بعد البلوغ. (مسكين) وعبيده للخدمة، وهذه الأشياء كلها يعتبر أن تكون مشغولة بحاجته الأصلية. (مسكين) عن نفسه متعلق بـ"تجب" أي يجب إخراجها عن نفسه. (ع)] بيان للسبب، والأصل فيه قوله عليا: "أدوا عن كل حر أو عبد، صغير أو كبير نصف صاع من بر، أو صاعاً من شعير"، وفي حديث الدار قطني عن من تمونون، ولا شك أن الإنسان يمون نفسه ويلي عليها، فيلحق به ما في معناه ممن يمونه كطفله الفقير وعبده كان العبد مسلماً أو كافراً، وقال الشافعي: لا تجب عن الكافر؛ لأنها تجب على العبد ابتداء، ثم للخدمة سواء
The Book of Zakat. Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr: On a free Muslim possessing a nisab surplus beyond his dwelling, clothes, furnishings, horse, and weapon. He is cautious against the slave, i.e., the owner, and his slaves for himself. Meaning: it is obligatory to pay it—and surplus beyond his clothes, i.e., his goods. Sadaqat al-Fitr is of the type of adding the thing to its condition. It was placed before fasting even though it is obligatory after it because it is a financial worship like zakat. 'Fitr' (with kasr) is a coined word, not Arabic nor Arabized, but a technical term of the jurists, thus a legal reality. Sadaqah is a gift intended for reward. Its pillar is payment to the recipient. The cause of its legislation is what came in the hadith of Ibn Abbas: 'The Messenger of Allah made Zakat al-Fitr obligatory as purification for the fasting person from vain talk and obscenity, and as food for the poor. Whoever pays it before the prayer, it is an accepted zakat; whoever pays it after the prayer, it is a charity.' He ordered it two days before Eid before zakat on wealth was made obligatory, and this is correct. Fasting and zakat were made obligatory in the second year of the Hijra, likewise the change of Qibla. (Fath) 'It is wajib': i.e., Sadaqat al-Fitr—contrary to al-Shafi'i, for whom it is fard. (Ayni) 'Upon a free Muslim': condition of freedom for valid ownership, condition of Islam for the charity to be an act of worship. Muhammad said: It is not obligatory upon the minor because it is worship, while they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say: It has the meaning of maintenance because he bears it on behalf of another, so it becomes like maintenance of relatives, unlike zakat because it is pure worship; therefore no one bears it. One (immature). And his statement: 'Possessor of nisab'—the attribute of growth is not considered. Al-Shafi'i said: It is obligatory upon one who possesses surplus beyond his daily sustenance, due to the aforementioned hadith of Ibn Abbas. Our proof is his statement (peace be upon him): 'No charity except from surplus wealth' and his statement: 'Surplus beyond his dwelling' because what is occupied with primary need is like non-existent. Among his primary needs is the dwelling house—so much so that if he has two houses, one he lives in and another he does not live in, renting it or not, its value is not considered in wealth; even if its value is two hundred dirhams, Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory on him. Likewise, what is surplus beyond his clothes, house goods, horse, and weapon used for religious need, and service slaves. For this reason they said: Books of tafsir, fiqh, and a single mushaf are not nisab because they are occupied with primary religious need. As for books of grammar, literature, medicine, and dream interpretation, they are considered nisab. (Miskin) 'Muslim': cautious against the disbeliever, whether the Muslim is young, old, or insane. Even if the guardian did not pay it, payment becomes obligatory after adulthood. (Miskin) And his service slaves—all these things are considered to be occupied with his primary need. (Miskin) 'For himself' is connected to 'is obligatory', i.e., it is obligatory to pay it for himself. (Ayni) Explanation of the cause, and its basis is his statement (Ali?): 'Pay on behalf of every free person or slave, young or old, half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley.' And in the hadith of al-Dar Qutni: 'On behalf of those whom you support.' There is no doubt that a person supports himself and manages himself, so what is in the same meaning—those whom he supports, like his poor child and his slave—is attached to him, whether the slave is Muslim or disbeliever. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not obligatory on behalf of the disbeliever because it is obligatory on the slave originally, then—for service, equally.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr (Zakat al-Fitr). The commentator explains the matn phrase by phrase. The ruling is that Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib (obligatory) upon every free Muslim who possesses a nisab surplus beyond his essential needs such as dwelling, clothing, furniture, horse, and weapon. The author clarifies the definition: 'Fitr' is a technical term meaning the fast-breaking charity. The pillar (rukn) of this act is payment to the eligible recipient. The cause for its legislation is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet ﷺ made it obligatory as purification for the fasting person and provision for the poor. The commentator notes a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers it fard (obligatory at the level of fard), while the Hanafi school holds it to be wajib—a distinct category between fard and sunnah. The conditions for obligation are freedom (to enable valid ownership) and Islam (for the act to be a worship). Within the Hanafi school, Imam Muhammad held that it is not wajib on a minor because it is an act of worship, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) consider it wajib on the minor because it contains the meaning of maintenance (mu'nah) which can be borne by the guardian. The nisab for Sadaqat al-Fitr does not require growth (nama'); it is sufficient to have wealth beyond basic needs. The commentator uses the hadith 'La sadaqata illa an zahri ghina' (No charity except from surplus wealth) to argue against al-Shafi'i's view that it is obligatory on anyone with surplus beyond one day's food. The text then details what items are excluded from nisab: dwelling, clothes, household goods, horse, weapons for religious need, and service slaves are considered primary needs. Even books of tafsir and fiqh are excluded, but books of grammar, literature, medicine, etc. are counted as nisab. The passage also states that the obligation applies only to Muslims; a disbeliever's slave is not subject to it? Actually, the text says 'Muslim' is a condition for the person paying; the recipient can be a disbeliever? The text later says the charity must be given to a Muslim. There is confusion in the OCR but the gist is clear.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (Sadaqat al-Fitr): The obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum wealth threshold exceeding basic needs (dwelling, clothes, etc.) triggering obligation of Sadaqat al-Fitr.
فضّل (fadala): Surplus beyond essential needs like housing, clothing, furniture, horse, weapon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal status (hukm) of Sadaqat al-Fitr?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah Mu'akkadah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib on every free Muslim who has surplus beyond essential needs; its proof is ahad, making it wajib not fard, and its nisab does not require growth.

On Whom Sadaqat al-Fitr is Paid: Dependents and Slaves
Details who must be paid for: poor minor children, service slaves, and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on who must receive Sadaqat al-Fitr from a person, and explain the key disputes with al-Shafi'i and intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib only for those under your full guardianship and maintenance—not for wife or adult child.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq specifies the categories for which Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory. The Hanafi school distinguishes between those over whom a person has guardianship (minor children, service slaves) and those without (wife, adult children). We will explore the reasoning and the disputes with al-Shafi'i, as well as intra-Hanafi differences on joint slaves and option purchases.

Guided Path
1
Identify wajib categories
List the individuals for whom fitrah is wajib: poor minor children, service slaves, mudabbar, umm walad.
Note that trade slaves are excluded.
2
Identify non-wajib categories
List those for whom fitrah is not wajib: wife, adult child, mukatab, grandparents.
Wife and adult child are not wajib but paying is permissible by istihsan.
3
Understand the illa
The cause is full guardianship (wilaya) and complete maintenance (nafaqa). For wife, only limited maintenance is required; for adult child, no guardianship.
This addresses the Shafi'i objection.
4
Analyze inter-madhhab dispute
Compare with Shafi'i who holds it wajib for wife and adult child based on the hadith 'Pay for those you support.'
Hanafi interpretation restricts 'support' to those under full guardianship.
5
Examine joint slave and option cases
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on joint slaves (Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn) and the suspension of obligation when a slave is bought with khiyar.
Practical differences arise in distribution.

Source Text

وطفله الفقير وعبيده للخدمة ومدبّره وأم ولده لا عن زوجته وولده الكبير ومكاتبه أي لا تجب على الرجل أي ولا عن أي تجب عن مديره في سم وعبد أو عبيد لهما، ويتوقف إلخ (فتح) الفقير: يعني أولاده الصغار الفقراء، فإن كان طفله غنيا يجب في ماله خلافاً لمحمد. (ع) وعبيده: أي عن عبيد الخدمة لا للتجارة خلافاً للشافعي (ع) أم ولده لما روى الدار قطني أنه عليه أمر بصدقة الفطر عن الكبير والحر والعبد ممن تمونون، وهؤلاء المذكورون بهذه الصفة على الكمال. (ع) لا عن زوجته لأنه لا يلي عليها ولا يمونها إلا لضرورة نظام مصالح النكاح، ولهذا لا يجب عليه غير الرواتب نحو الأدوية، وقوله: وولده الكبير؛ لأنه لا يمونه ولا يلي عليه، فانعدم السبب، وكذا إن كان في عياله لعدم الولاية عليه، إلا أن يكون مجنونا، ولو أدى عن الزوجة والولد الكبير جاز استحساناً؛ لثبوت الإذن عادة، ولا يؤدي عن أجداده وجداته؛ لأنهم ليسوا في معنى نفسه خلافاً للشافعي فيهما أي في الزوجة والولد الكبير؛ لقوله عليه: "أدوا عن من تمونون"، ولنا ما سبق من أن السبب رأس يمونه ويلي عليه ولا ولاية له عليهما. (فتح) وعبد أو عبيد :لهما أما العبد المشترك فلا تجب عنه عندنا على أحد من الشريكين؛ لقصور الولاية والمؤنة في حق كل واحد من السيدين وفيه خلاف الشافعي بناء على أصله من أنها تجب على العبد ابتداء ثم يتحملها المولى عنه، والعبد ههنا كامل في نفسه وهما يمونانه فتجب عليهما، وأما العبيد المشتركة فعندهما على كل واحد منهما ما يخصه من الرؤوس دون الأشقاص حتى لو كان بينهما خمسة أعبد يجب على كل واحد منهما الصدقة عن عبدين؛ لأنه باعتبار القسمة يكون ملك كل واحد من السيدين كاملا في عبدين ولا يعتبر النصف الخامس، والأصل في هذا أن الإمام لا يرى قسمة الرقيق وهما يريانها يعني أن أبا حنيفة لا يرى قسمة الرقيق جبراً فلا يملك كل واحد منهما عبداً معينا، بل ملكهما شائع في الكل، وقيل: لا تجب إجماعا؛ لأن النصيب لا يجتمع قبل القسمة فلم تتم الرقبة بواحد منهما. (فتح) ويتوقف إلخ أي لو اشترى عبدا بالخيار ومر وقت الفطر، والخيار باق ففطرته على من يستقر الملك له، بخلاف النفقة فإنها تجب على من كان له الملك وقت الوجوب لعدم احتمالها التوقف؛ لأنها تجب لحاجة المملوك للحال، فلو جعلناها موقوفا لمات جوعا وعند زفر على من له الخيار ؛ لأن الولاية له، وقال الشافعي على من له الملك وقت الوجوب كالنفقة ولنا أن الملك موقوف؛ لأنه لو رد يعود إلى قديم ملك البائع، ولو أجيز يثبت الملك للمشتري من وقت العقد فيتوقف ما يبتني عليه بخلاف النفقة فإنها للحال الناجزة فلا تقبل التوقف. (فتح)
And his poor child, and his service slaves, and his mudabbar, and his umm walad—not for his wife, his adult child, and his mukatab. Meaning: It is not obligatory for the man, nor for—any (?) obligatory for his mudabbar in ? and joint slave or slaves for both, and it is suspended etc. (Fath) 'al-Faqir': meaning his poor minor children. If his child is wealthy, it is obligatory from his wealth, contrary to Muhammad. (Ayni) 'And his slaves': meaning service slaves, not for trade, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (Ayni) 'His umm walad' because al-Dar Qutni narrated that he ﷺ ordered Sadaqat al-Fitr on behalf of the adult, free, and slave from those you support. These mentioned with this description are complete. (Ayni) 'Not for his wife' because he does not have guardianship over her nor does he support her except out of necessity for the system of marital interests; therefore, he is not obligated for non-routine expenses like medicine. And his statement: 'And his adult child' because he does not support him nor has guardianship over him, so the cause is absent. Likewise even if he is in his household due to lack of guardianship, unless he is insane. If he pays for the wife and adult child, it is permissible by istihsan due to customary permission. He does not pay for his grandfathers and grandmothers because they are not in the same meaning as himself, contrary to al-Shafi'i regarding both (wife and adult child) due to his statement: 'Pay on behalf of those you support.' Our proof is what preceded: that the cause is a person whom he supports and over whom he has guardianship, and he has no guardianship over them. (Fath) 'And joint slave or slaves for both': As for a jointly owned slave, it is not obligatory upon either partner in our view, due to the deficiency of guardianship and maintenance on the part of each master. There is a difference with al-Shafi'i based on his principle that it is obligatory on the slave originally, then the master bears it for him. Here the slave is complete in himself and both support him, so it is obligatory on both. As for jointly owned female slaves, according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) each is obligated for what corresponds to his share of whole slaves, not fractions, so that if they have five slaves, each must pay for two slaves, because by division each master's ownership becomes complete in two slaves, and the half of the fifth is not considered. The basis is that Abu Hanifa does not consider division of slaves compulsory, so each does not own a specific slave; rather, their ownership is shared in the whole. It is said: It is not obligatory by consensus because the share is not unified before division, so the slave is not wholly possessed by either. (Fath) 'And it is suspended etc.': If someone bought a slave with an option and the time of Fitr passed while the option remains, his fitrah is on the one to whom ownership will be settled, unlike maintenance which is obligatory on the one who had ownership at the time of obligation because it cannot bear suspension, as it is for the immediate need of the slave; if we made it suspended, he would die of hunger. According to Zufar, it is on the one who has the option because he has guardianship. Al-Shafi'i says on the one who had ownership at the time of obligation like maintenance. Our proof is that ownership is suspended; if he rejects, it returns to the seller's original ownership; if he accepts, ownership is established for the buyer from the time of contract, so what is built on it is suspended, unlike maintenance which is for the immediate present and cannot bear suspension.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr, specifying for whom a person must pay. The ruling is that a person must pay for: his poor minor children, his service slaves (not trade slaves), his mudabbar (slave promised freedom after the master's death), and his umm walad (concubine who bore his child). He does not have to pay for his wife, his adult child, or his mukatab (slave under a contract of manumission). The text explains the reasons: he has guardianship and maintenance over those he must pay for (himself, minor children, slaves, etc.), but not over his wife (he does not fully support her beyond necessities) nor over his adult child (even if they live in his house). Paying for wife and adult child is nonetheless permissible by istihsan due to custom. The passage then discusses joint slaves: if two partners share a slave, neither is obligated because neither has complete guardianship or full maintenance responsibility. For jointly owned female slaves (or multiple slaves), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that each partner pays according to his share of whole slaves, not fractions (e.g., if they own 5 slaves, each pays for 2, ignoring the odd half). Abu Hanifa, however, does not consider division of slaves compulsory, so ownership is shared in the whole, and thus no specific obligation on either. The text also covers the case of buying a slave with an option (khiyar): the fitrah is suspended until ownership is settled; if the option is exercised to cancel, the seller bears it; if confirmed, the buyer bears it from the time of contract. This differs from maintenance which must be immediate. Disputes with Zufar and al-Shafi'i are noted.

Key Terms
مدبّر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) to buy his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, for which of the following must a man pay Sadaqat al-Fitr?
A) His wife
B) His adult son living in his house
C) His poor minor child
D) His father

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib only for those whom a person fully supports and over whom he has guardianship: minor children, service slaves, mudabbar, and umm walad.
